</td></tr></tbody></table>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Marriage">Marriage</span></h3>
<p>In the Incan Empire, the age of marriage differed for men and women: men typically married at the age of 20, while women usually got married about four years earlier at the age of 16.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_47-0"><a href="#cite_note-:0-47">[46]</a></sup> Men who were highly ranked in society could have multiple wives, but those lower in the ranks could only take a single wife.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48"><a href="#cite_note-48">[47]</a></sup> Marriages were typically within classes and resembled a more business-like agreement. Once married, the women were expected to cook, collect food and watch over the children and livestock.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_47-1"><a href="#cite_note-:0-47">[46]</a></sup> Girls and mothers would also work around the house to keep it orderly to please the public inspectors.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49"><a href="#cite_note-49">[48]</a></sup> These duties remained the same even after wives became pregnant and with the added responsibility of praying and making offerings to Kanopa, who was the god of pregnancy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_47-2"><a href="#cite_note-:0-47">[46]</a></sup> It was typical for marriages to begin on a trial basis with both men and women having a say in the longevity of the marriage. If the man felt that it wouldn't work out or if the woman wanted to return to her parents' home the marriage would end. Once the marriage was final, the only way the two could be divorced was if they did not have a child together.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_47-3"><a href="#cite_note-:0-47">[46]</a></sup> Marriage within the Empire was crucial for survival. A family was considered disadvantaged if there was not a married couple at the center because everyday life centered around the balance of male and female tasks.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:3_50-0"><a href="#cite_note-:3-50">[49]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Gender_roles">Gender roles</span></h3>
<p>According to some historians, such as Terence N. D'Altroy, male and female roles were considered equal in Inca society. The "indigenous cultures saw the two genders as complementary parts of a whole".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:3_50-1"><a href="#cite_note-:3-50">[49]</a></sup> In other words, there was not a hierarchical structure in the domestic sphere for the Incas. Within the domestic sphere, women came to be known as weavers, although there is significant evidence to suggest that this gender role did not appear until colonizing Spaniards realized women's productive talents in this sphere and used it to their economic advantage. There is evidence to suggest that both men and women contributed equally to the weaving tasks in pre-Hispanic Andean culture.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:4_51-0"><a href="#cite_note-:4-51">[50]</a></sup> Women's everyday tasks included: spinning, watching the children, weaving cloth, cooking, brewing chichi, preparing fields for cultivation, planting seeds, bearing children, harvesting, weeding, hoeing, herding, and carrying water.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:2_52-0"><a href="#cite_note-:2-52">[51]</a></sup> Men on the other hand, "weeded, plowed, participated in combat, helped in the harvest, carried firewood, built houses, herded llama and alpaca, and spun and wove when necessary".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:2_52-1"><a href="#cite_note-:2-52">[51]</a></sup> This relationship between the genders may have been complementary. Unsurprisingly, onlooking Spaniards believed women were treated like slaves, because women did not work in Spanish society to the same extent, and certainly did not work in fields.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53"><a href="#cite_note-53">[52]</a></sup>  Women were sometimes allowed to own land and herds because inheritance was passed down from both the mother's and father's side of the family.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54"><a href="#cite_note-54">[53]</a></sup> Kinship within the Inca society followed a parallel line of descent. In other words, women descended from women and men descended from men. Due to the parallel descent, a woman had access to land and other assets through her mother.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:2_52-2"><a href="#cite_note-:2-52">[51]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Burial_customs">Burial customs</span></h3>
<p>Due to the dry climate that extends from Peru to northern Chile, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mummification" title="Mummification">mummification</a> occurred naturally by <a href="/wiki/Desiccation" title="Desiccation">desiccation.</a> It is believed that the ancient Incas learned to mummify their dead to show reverence to their leaders and representatives. <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-55"><a href="#cite_note-55">[54]</a></sup> Mummification was chosen to preserve the body and to give others the opportunity to worship them in their death. The ancient Inca believed in reincarnation, so preservation of the body was vital for passage into the afterlife. <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56"><a href="#cite_note-56">[55]</a></sup> Since mummification was reserved for royalty, this entailed preserving power by placing the deceased's valuables with the body in places of honor. The bodies remained accessible for ceremonies where they would be removed and celebrated with. <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57"><a href="#cite_note-57">[56]</a></sup> The ancient Inca mummified their dead with various tools. <a href="/wiki/Chicha" title="Chicha">Chicha</a> corn beer was used to delay <a href="/wiki/Decomposition" title="Decomposition">decomposition</a> and the effects of bacterial activity on the body. The bodies were then stuffed with natural materials such as vegetable matter and animal hair. Sticks were to used to maintain their shape and poses.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-58"><a href="#cite_note-58">[57]</a></sup> In addition to the mummification process, the Inca would bury their dead in the fetal position inside a vessel intended to mimic the womb for preparation of their new birth. A ceremony would be held that included music, food, and drink for the relatives and loved ones of the deceased. <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-59"><a href="#cite_note-59">[58]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Religion">Religion</span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Religion_in_the_Inca_Empire" title="Religion in the Inca Empire">Religion in the Inca Empire</a> and <a href="/wiki/Inca_mythology" title="Inca mythology">Inca mythology</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Cabeza_de_Viracocha,_Museo_de_Am%C3%A9rica.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2816" data-file-width="2112" decoding="async" height="227" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f0/Cabeza_de_Viracocha%2C_Museo_de_Am%C3%A9rica.jpg/170px-Cabeza_de_Viracocha%2C_Museo_de_Am%C3%A9rica.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f0/Cabeza_de_Viracocha%2C_Museo_de_Am%C3%A9rica.jpg/255px-Cabeza_de_Viracocha%2C_Museo_de_Am%C3%A9rica.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f0/Cabeza_de_Viracocha%2C_Museo_de_Am%C3%A9rica.jpg/340px-Cabeza_de_Viracocha%2C_Museo_de_Am%C3%A9rica.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Cabeza_de_Viracocha,_Museo_de_Am%C3%A9rica.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Diorite <a href="/wiki/Viracocha" title="Viracocha">Viracocha</a> Inca sculpture from Amarucancha archeological site, <a href="/wiki/Cusco" title="Cusco">Cusco</a></div></div></div>
<p>Inca myths were <a href="/wiki/Oral_tradition" title="Oral tradition">transmitted orally</a> until early Spanish colonists recorded them; however, some scholars claim that they were recorded on quipus, Andean knotted string records.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Urton2009_60-0"><a href="#cite_note-Urton2009-60">[59]</a></sup>
</p><p>The Inca believed in <a href="/wiki/Reincarnation" title="Reincarnation">reincarnation</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-61"><a href="#cite_note-61">[60]</a></sup> After death, the passage to the next world was fraught with difficulties. The spirit of the dead, <i>camaquen,</i> would need to follow a long road and during the trip the assistance of a black dog that could see in the dark was required. Most Incas imagined the after world to be like an earthly paradise with flower-covered fields and snow-capped mountains.
</p><p>It was important to the Inca that they not die as a result of burning or that the body of the deceased not be incinerated. Burning would cause their vital force to disappear and threaten their passage to the after world. The Inca nobility practiced <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cranial_deformation" title="Cranial deformation">cranial deformation</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BurgerSalazar2004_62-0"><a href="#cite_note-BurgerSalazar2004-62">[61]</a></sup> They wrapped tight cloth straps around the heads of newborns to shape their soft skulls into a more conical form, thus distinguishing the nobility from other social classes.
</p><p>The Incas made <a href="/wiki/Human_sacrifice" title="Human sacrifice">human sacrifices</a>. As many as 4,000 servants, court officials, favorites and concubines were killed upon the death of the Inca <a href="/wiki/Huayna_Capac" title="Huayna Capac">Huayna Capac</a> in 1527.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Davies1981_63-0"><a href="#cite_note-Davies1981-63">[62]</a></sup> The Incas performed child sacrifices around important events, such as the death of the Sapa Inca or during a famine. These sacrifices were known as <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Qhapaq_hucha" title="Qhapaq hucha">qhapaq hucha</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-64"><a href="#cite_note-64">[63]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Deities">Deities</span></h3>
<p>The Incas were <a href="/wiki/Polytheism" title="Polytheism">polytheists</a> who worshipped many gods. These included:
</p>
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Viracocha" title="Viracocha">Viracocha</a> (also Pachacamac) – Created all living things</li>
<li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Apu_Illapu" title="Apu Illapu">Apu Illapu</a> – Rain God, prayed to when they need rain</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Ayar_Cachi" title="Ayar Cachi">Ayar Cachi</a> – Hot-tempered God, causes earthquakes</li>
<li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Illapa" title="Illapa">Illapa</a> – Goddess of lightning and thunder (also Yakumama water goddess)</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Inti" title="Inti">Inti</a> – sun god and patron deity of the holy city of Cusco (home of the sun)</li>
<li><a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Kuychi&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Kuychi (page does not exist)">Kuychi</a> – Rainbow God, connected with fertility</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Mama_Killa" title="Mama Killa">Mama Killa</a> – Wife of Inti, called Moon Mother</li>
<li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mama_Occlo" title="Mama Occlo">Mama Occlo</a> – Wisdom to civilize the people, taught women to weave cloth and build houses</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Manco_C%C3%A1pac" title="Manco Cápac">Manco Cápac</a> – known for his courage and sent to earth to become first king of the Incas. Taught people how to grow plants, make weapons, work together, share resources and worship the Gods</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Pachamama" title="Pachamama">Pachamama</a> – The Goddess of earth and wife of Viracocha. People give her offerings of coca leaves and beer and pray to her for major agricultural occasions</li>
<li>Quchamama – Goddess of the sea</li>
<li>Sachamama – Means Mother Tree, goddess in the shape of a snake with two heads</li>
<li>Yakumama – Means mother Water. Represented as a snake. When she came to earth she transformed into a great river (also Illapa).</li></ul>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Economy">Economy</span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Economy_of_the_Inca_Empire" title="Economy of the Inca Empire">Economy of the Inca Empire</a></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Incan_agriculture" title="Incan agriculture">Incan agriculture</a>, <a href="/wiki/Vertical_archipelago" title="Vertical archipelago">Vertical archipelago</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mit%27a" title="Mit'a">Mit'a</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Qullqa" title="Qullqa">Qullqa</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Trabajo-inca8.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="565" data-file-width="414" decoding="async" height="232" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e1/Trabajo-inca8.jpg/170px-Trabajo-inca8.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e1/Trabajo-inca8.jpg/255px-Trabajo-inca8.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e1/Trabajo-inca8.jpg/340px-Trabajo-inca8.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Trabajo-inca8.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Illustration of <a href="/wiki/Incan_agriculture" title="Incan agriculture">Inca farmers</a> using a <i>chakitaqlla</i> (Andean foot plough), <a href="/wiki/Felipe_Guaman_Poma_de_Ayala" title="Felipe Guaman Poma de Ayala">Felipe Guaman Poma de Ayala</a>, <i><a href="/wiki/El_primer_nueva_cor%C3%B3nica_y_buen_gobierno" title="El primer nueva corónica y buen gobierno">El primer nueva corónica y buen gobierno</a></i>, <i>circa</i> 1615</div></div></div>
<p>The Inca Empire employed <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Central_planning" title="Central planning">central planning</a>. The Inca Empire traded with outside regions, although they did not operate a substantial internal <a href="/wiki/Market_economy" title="Market economy">market economy</a>. While <a href="/wiki/Axe-monies" title="Axe-monies">axe-monies</a> were used along the northern coast, presumably by the provincial <i>mindaláe</i> trading class,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-65"><a href="#cite_note-65">[64]</a></sup> most households in the empire lived in a <a href="/wiki/Traditional_economy" title="Traditional economy">traditional economy</a> in which households were required to pay taxes, usually in the form of the <i><a href="/wiki/Mit%27a" title="Mit'a">mit'a</a></i> corvée labor, and military obligations,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-66"><a href="#cite_note-66">[65]</a></sup> though barter (or <i>trueque</i>) was present in some areas.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMoseley200144_67-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMoseley200144-67">[66]</a></sup> In return, the state provided security, food in times of hardship through the supply of emergency resources, agricultural projects (e.g. aqueducts and terraces) to increase productivity, and occasional feasts hosted by Inca officials for their subjects. While <i>mit'a</i> was used by the state to obtain labor, individual villages had a pre-inca system of communal work, known as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mink%27a" title="Mink'a">mink'a</a>. This system survives to the modern day, known as <i>mink'a</i> or <i>faena</i>. The economy rested on the material foundations of the <a href="/wiki/Vertical_archipelago" title="Vertical archipelago">vertical archipelago</a>, a system of ecological complementarity in accessing resources<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-68"><a href="#cite_note-68">[67]</a></sup> and the cultural foundation of <i><a href="/wiki/Ayni" title="Ayni">ayni</a></i>, or <a href="/wiki/Reciprocity_(cultural_anthropology)" title="Reciprocity (cultural anthropology)">reciprocal exchange</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-69"><a href="#cite_note-69">[68]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-70"><a href="#cite_note-70">[69]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Government">Government</span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Government_of_the_Inca_Empire" title="Government of the Inca Empire">Government of the Inca Empire</a></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Beliefs"><span class="anchor" id="Beliefs"></span>Beliefs</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Jos%C3%A9_Bernardo_de_Tagle_Inti.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="400" data-file-width="400" decoding="async" height="170" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2a/Jos%C3%A9_Bernardo_de_Tagle_Inti.svg/170px-Jos%C3%A9_Bernardo_de_Tagle_Inti.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2a/Jos%C3%A9_Bernardo_de_Tagle_Inti.svg/255px-Jos%C3%A9_Bernardo_de_Tagle_Inti.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2a/Jos%C3%A9_Bernardo_de_Tagle_Inti.svg/340px-Jos%C3%A9_Bernardo_de_Tagle_Inti.svg.png 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Jos%C3%A9_Bernardo_de_Tagle_Inti.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Inti" title="Inti">Inti</a>, as represented by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Jos%C3%A9_Bernardo_de_Tagle" title="José Bernardo de Tagle">José Bernardo de Tagle</a> of Peru</div></div></div>
<p>The Sapa Inca was conceptualized as divine and was effectively head of the state religion. The <i><a href="/wiki/Willaq_Umu" title="Willaq Umu">Willaq Umu</a></i> (or Chief Priest) was second to the emperor. Local religious traditions continued and in some cases such as the Oracle at <a href="/wiki/Pachacamac" title="Pachacamac">Pachacamac</a> on the Peruvian coast, were officially venerated. Following Pachacuti, the Sapa Inca claimed descent from Inti, who placed a high value on imperial blood; by the end of the empire, it was common to <a href="/wiki/Incest" title="Incest">incestuously</a> wed brother and sister. He was "son of the sun", and his people the <i>intip churin</i>, or "children of the sun", and both his right to rule and mission to conquer derived from his holy ancestor. The Sapa Inca also presided over ideologically important festivals, notably during the <i><a href="/wiki/Inti_Raymi" title="Inti Raymi">Inti Raymi</a></i>, or "Sunfest" attended by soldiers, mummified rulers, nobles, clerics and the general population of Cusco beginning on the June solstice and culminating nine days later with the ritual breaking of the earth using a foot plow by the Inca. Moreover, Cusco was considered cosmologically central, loaded as it was with <i>huacas</i> and radiating <i>ceque</i> lines as the geographic center of the Four-Quarters; <a href="/wiki/Inca_Garcilaso_de_la_Vega" title="Inca Garcilaso de la Vega">Inca Garcilaso de la Vega</a> called it "the navel of the universe".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-71"><a href="#cite_note-71">[70]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTED'Altroy201486–89,_111,_154–55_72-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTED'Altroy201486–89,_111,_154–55-72">[71]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMoseley200181–85_73-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMoseley200181–85-73">[72]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcEwan2008138–39_74-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcEwan2008138–39-74">[73]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Organization_of_the_empire">Organization of the empire</span></h3>
<p>The Inca Empire was a <a href="/wiki/Federation" title="Federation">federalist system</a> consisting of a central government with the Inca at its head and four regional quarters, or <i>suyu</i>: <a href="/wiki/Chinchay_Suyu" title="Chinchay Suyu">Chinchay Suyu</a> (NW), <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Anti_Suyu" title="Anti Suyu">Anti Suyu</a> (NE), <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kunti_Suyu" title="Kunti Suyu">Kunti Suyu</a> (SW) and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Qulla_Suyu" title="Qulla Suyu">Qulla Suyu</a> (SE). The four corners of these quarters met at the center, Cusco. These <i>suyu</i> were likely created around 1460 during the reign of Pachacuti before the empire reached its largest territorial extent. At the time the <i>suyu</i> were established they were roughly of equal size and only later changed their proportions as the empire expanded north and south along the Andes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-75"><a href="#cite_note-75">[74]</a></sup>
</p><p>Cusco was likely not organized as a <i>wamani</i>, or province. Rather, it was probably somewhat akin to a modern <a href="/wiki/Federal_district" title="Federal district">federal district</a>, like Washington, DC or Mexico City. The city sat at the center of the four <i>suyu</i> and served as the preeminent center of politics and religion. While Cusco was essentially governed by the Sapa Inca, his relatives and the royal <i>panaqa</i> lineages, each <i>suyu</i> was governed by an <i>Apu</i>, a term of esteem used for men of high status and for venerated mountains. Both Cusco as a district and the four <i>suyu</i> as administrative regions were grouped into upper <i>hanan</i> and lower <i>hurin</i> divisions. As the Inca did not have written records, it is impossible to exhaustively list the constituent <i>wamani</i>. However, colonial records allow us to reconstruct a partial list. There were likely more than 86 <i>wamani</i>, with more than 48 in the highlands and more than 38 on the coast.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-76"><a href="#cite_note-76">[75]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTED'Altroy201442–43,_86–89_77-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTED'Altroy201442–43,_86–89-77">[76]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcEwan2008113–14_78-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcEwan2008113–14-78">[77]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Suyu">Suyu</span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Inca_Empire_South_America.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="590" data-file-width="517" decoding="async" height="194" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/44/Inca_Empire_South_America.png/170px-Inca_Empire_South_America.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/44/Inca_Empire_South_America.png/255px-Inca_Empire_South_America.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/44/Inca_Empire_South_America.png/340px-Inca_Empire_South_America.png 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Inca_Empire_South_America.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The four <i>suyus</i> or quarters of the empire.</div></div></div>
<p>The most populous <i>suyu</i> was Chinchaysuyu, which encompassed the former Chimu empire and much of the northern Andes. At its largest extent, it extended through much of modern Ecuador and into modern Colombia.
</p><p>The largest <i>suyu</i> by area was Qullasuyu, named after the <a href="/wiki/Aymara_language" title="Aymara language">Aymara</a>-speaking <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Qulla_people" title="Qulla people">Qulla people</a>. It encompassed the Bolivian <a href="/wiki/Altiplano" title="Altiplano">Altiplano</a> and much of the southern Andes, reaching Argentina and as far south as the <a href="/wiki/Maipo_River" title="Maipo River">Maipo</a> or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Maule_river" title="Maule river">Maule river</a> in <a href="/wiki/Incas_in_Central_Chile" title="Incas in Central Chile">Central Chile</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dillehay1988_79-0"><a href="#cite_note-Dillehay1988-79">[78]</a></sup> Historian <a href="/wiki/Jos%C3%A9_Bengoa" title="José Bengoa">José Bengoa</a> singled out <a href="/wiki/Quillota" title="Quillota">Quillota</a> as likely being the foremost Inca settlement in Chile.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBengoa200337–38_80-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBengoa200337–38-80">[79]</a></sup>
</p><p>The second smallest <i>suyu</i>, Antisuyu, was northwest of Cusco in the high Andes. Its name is the root of the word "Andes".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTED'Altroy201487_81-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTED'Altroy201487-81">[80]</a></sup>
</p><p>Kuntisuyu was the smallest <i>suyu</i>, located along the southern coast of modern Peru, extending into the highlands towards Cusco.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTED'Altroy201487–88_82-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTED'Altroy201487–88-82">[81]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Laws">Laws</span></h3>
<p>The Inca state had no <a href="/wiki/Judicial_independence" title="Judicial independence">separate judiciary</a> or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Code_(law)" title="Code (law)">codified laws</a>. Customs, expectations and traditional local power holders governed behavior. The state had legal force, such as through <i>tokoyrikoq</i> (lit. "he who sees all"), or inspectors. The highest such inspector, typically a blood relative to the Sapa Inca, acted independently of the conventional hierarchy, providing a point of view for the Sapa Inca free of bureaucratic influence.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTED'Altroy2014235–36_83-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTED'Altroy2014235–36-83">[82]</a></sup>
</p><p>The Inca had three moral precepts that governed their behavior:
</p>
<ul><li><i>Ama sua</i>: Do not steal</li>
<li><i>Ama llulla</i>: Do not lie</li>
<li><i>Ama quella</i>: Do not be lazy</li></ul>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Administration">Administration</span></h3>
<p>Colonial sources are not entirely clear or in agreement about Inca government structure, such as exact duties and functions of government positions. But the basic structure can be broadly described. The top was the <i>Sapa Inca</i>. Below that may have been the <i>Willaq Umu</i>, literally the "priest who recounts", the High Priest of the Sun.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTED'Altroy201499_84-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTED'Altroy201499-84">[83]</a></sup> However, beneath the <i>Sapa Inca</i> also sat the <i>Inkap rantin</i>, who was a confidant and assistant to the <i>Sapa Inca</i>, perhaps similar to a Prime Minister.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-85"><a href="#cite_note-85">[84]</a></sup> Starting with <a href="/wiki/Topa_Inca_Yupanqui" title="Topa Inca Yupanqui">Topa Inca Yupanqui</a>, a "Council of the Realm" was composed of 16 nobles: 2 from <i>hanan</i> Cusco; 2 from <i>hurin</i> Cusco; 4 from Chinchaysuyu; 2 from Cuntisuyu; 4 from Collasuyu; and 2 from Antisuyu. This weighting of representation balanced the <i>hanan</i> and <i>hurin</i> divisions of the empire, both within Cusco and within the Quarters (<i>hanan suyukuna</i> and <i>hurin suyukuna</i>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEZuidema198348_86-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEZuidema198348-86">[85]</a></sup>
</p><p>While provincial <a href="/wiki/Bureaucracy" title="Bureaucracy">bureaucracy</a> and government varied greatly, the basic organization was decimal. Taxpayers – male heads of household of a certain age range – were organized into <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Corv%C3%A9e_labor" title="Corvée labor">corvée labor</a> units (often doubling as military units) that formed the state's muscle as part of <a href="/wiki/Mit%27a" title="Mit'a">mit'a</a> service. Each unit of more than 100 tax-payers were headed by a <i>kuraka</i>, while smaller units were headed by a <i>kamayuq</i>, a lower, non-hereditary status. However, while <i>kuraka</i> status was hereditary and typically served for life, the position of a <i>kuraka</i> in the hierarchy was subject to change based on the privileges of superiors in the hierarchy; a <i>pachaka kuraka</i> could be appointed to the position by a <i>waranqa kuraka</i>. Furthermore, one <i>kuraka</i> in each decimal level could serve as the head of one of the nine groups at a lower level, so that a <i>pachaka kuraka</i> might also be a <i>waranqa kuraka</i>, in effect directly responsible for one unit of 100 tax-payers and less directly responsible for nine other such units.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJulien1982121–27_87-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJulien1982121–27-87">[86]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTED'Altroy2014233–34_88-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTED'Altroy2014233–34-88">[87]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcEwan2008114–15_89-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcEwan2008114–15-89">[88]</a></sup>
</p>
<table class="wikitable">
<tbody><tr>
<th>Kuraka in Charge<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJulien1982123_90-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJulien1982123-90">[89]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTED'Altroy2014233_91-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTED'Altroy2014233-91">[90]</a></sup></th>

</th></tr>

</td></tr>
<tr>

</td></tr>
<tr>

<td>1,000
</td></tr>
<tr>

</td></tr>
<tr>

</td></tr>

</td></tr>
<tr>

</td></tr></tbody></table>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Arts_and_technology">Arts and technology</span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Monumental_architecture">Monumental architecture</span></h3>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1062260506">.mw-parser-output .quotebox{background-color:#F9F9F9;border:1px solid #aaa;box-sizing:border-box;padding:10px;font-size:88%;max-width:100%}.mw-parser-output .quotebox.floatleft{margin:.5em 1.4em .8em 0}.mw-parser-output .quotebox.floatright{margin:.5em 0 .8em 1.4em}.mw-parser-output .quotebox.centered{overflow:hidden;position:relative;margin:.5em auto .8em auto}.mw-parser-output .quotebox.floatleft span,.mw-parser-output .quotebox.floatright span{font-style:inherit}.mw-parser-output .quotebox>blockquote{margin:0;padding:0;border-left:0;font-family:inherit;font-size:inherit}.mw-parser-output .quotebox-title{background-color:#F9F9F9;text-align:center;font-size:110%;font-weight:bold}.mw-parser-output .quotebox-quote>:first-child{margin-top:0}.mw-parser-output .quotebox-quote:last-child>:last-child{margin-bottom:0}.mw-parser-output .quotebox-quote.quoted:before{font-family:"Times New Roman",serif;font-weight:bold;font-size:large;color:gray;content:" “ ";vertical-align:-45%;line-height:0}.mw-parser-output .quotebox-quote.quoted:after{font-family:"Times New Roman",serif;font-weight:bold;font-size:large;color:gray;content:" ” ";line-height:0}.mw-parser-output .quotebox .left-aligned{text-align:left}.mw-parser-output .quotebox .right-aligned{text-align:right}.mw-parser-output .quotebox .center-aligned{text-align:center}.mw-parser-output .quotebox .quote-title,.mw-parser-output .quotebox .quotebox-quote{display:block}.mw-parser-output .quotebox cite{display:block;font-style:normal}@media screen and (max-width:640px){.mw-parser-output .quotebox{width:100%!important;margin:0 0 .8em!important;float:none!important}}</style><div class="quotebox pullquote floatright" style="width:25em; ;">
<blockquote class="quotebox-quote left-aligned" style="">
<p>We can assure your majesty that it is so beautiful and has such fine buildings that it would even be remarkable in Spain.
</p>
</blockquote>
<p><cite class="left-aligned" style=""><a href="/wiki/Francisco_Pizarro" title="Francisco Pizarro">Francisco Pizarro</a></cite>
</p>
</div>
<p>Architecture was the most important of the Incan arts, with textiles reflecting architectural motifs. The most notable example is <a href="/wiki/Machu_Picchu" title="Machu Picchu">Machu Picchu</a>, which was constructed by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Inca_engineers" title="Inca engineers">Inca engineers</a>. The prime Inca structures were made of stone blocks that fit together so well that a knife could not be fitted through the stonework. These constructs have survived for centuries, with no use of mortar to sustain them.
</p><p>This process was first used on a large scale by the <a href="/wiki/Pukara" title="Pukara">Pucara</a> (c. 300 BC–AD 300) peoples to the south in <a href="/wiki/Lake_Titicaca" title="Lake Titicaca">Lake Titicaca</a> and later in the city of <a href="/wiki/Tiwanaku" title="Tiwanaku">Tiwanaku</a> (c. AD 400–1100) in present-day Bolivia. The rocks were sculpted to fit together exactly by repeatedly lowering a rock onto another and carving away any sections on the lower rock where the dust was compressed. The tight fit and the concavity on the lower rocks made them extraordinarily stable, despite the ongoing challenge of earthquakes and volcanic activity.
</p>
<h3><span id="Measures.2C_calendrics_and_mathematics"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Measures,_calendrics_and_mathematics">Measures, calendrics and mathematics</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Mathematics_of_the_Incas" title="Mathematics of the Incas">Mathematics of the Incas</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Inca._Quipu.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1152" data-file-width="1536" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8b/Inca._Quipu.jpg/220px-Inca._Quipu.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8b/Inca._Quipu.jpg/330px-Inca._Quipu.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8b/Inca._Quipu.jpg/440px-Inca._Quipu.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Inca._Quipu.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Quipu, 15th century. <a href="/wiki/Brooklyn_Museum" title="Brooklyn Museum">Brooklyn Museum</a></div></div></div>
<p>Physical measures used by the Inca were based on human body parts. Units included fingers, the distance from thumb to forefinger, palms, <a href="/wiki/Cubit" title="Cubit">cubits</a> and wingspans. The most basic distance unit was <i>thatkiy</i> or <i>thatki</i>, or one pace. The next largest unit was reported by Cobo to be the <i>topo</i> or <i>tupu</i>, measuring 6,000 <i>thatkiy</i>s, or about 7.7 km (4.8 mi); careful study has shown that a range of 4.0 to 6.3 km (2.5 to 3.9 mi) is likely. Next was the <i>wamani</i>, composed of 30 <i>topo</i>s (roughly 232 km or 144 mi). To measure area, 25 by 50 wingspans were used, reckoned in <i>topo</i>s (roughly 3,280 km<sup>2</sup> or 1,270 sq mi). It seems likely that distance was often interpreted as one day's walk; the distance between <i>tambo</i> way-stations varies widely in terms of distance, but far less in terms of time to walk that distance.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTED'Altroy2014246–47_92-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTED'Altroy2014246–47-92">[91]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcEwan2008179–80_93-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcEwan2008179–80-93">[92]</a></sup>
</p><p>Inca <a href="/wiki/Calendar" title="Calendar">calendars</a> were strongly tied to <a href="/wiki/Astronomy" title="Astronomy">astronomy</a>. Inca astronomers understood <a href="/wiki/Equinox" title="Equinox">equinoxes</a>, <a href="/wiki/Solstice" title="Solstice">solstices</a> and <a href="/wiki/Zenith" title="Zenith">zenith</a> passages, along with the <a href="/wiki/Transit_of_Venus" title="Transit of Venus">Venus cycle</a>. They could not, however, predict <a href="/wiki/Eclipse" title="Eclipse">eclipses</a>. The Inca calendar was essentially <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lunisolar" title="Lunisolar">lunisolar</a>, as two calendars were maintained in parallel, one <a href="/wiki/Solar_calendar" title="Solar calendar">solar</a> and one <a href="/wiki/Lunar_calendar" title="Lunar calendar">lunar</a>. As 12 lunar months fall 11 days short of a full 365-day solar year, those in charge of the calendar had to adjust every winter solstice. Each lunar month was marked with festivals and rituals.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTED'Altroy2014150–54_94-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTED'Altroy2014150–54-94">[93]</a></sup> Apparently, the days of the week were not named and days were not grouped into weeks. Similarly, months were not grouped into seasons. Time during a day was not measured in hours or minutes, but in terms of how far the sun had travelled or in how long it had taken to perform a task.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcEwan2008185–87_95-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcEwan2008185–87-95">[94]</a></sup>
</p><p>The sophistication of Inca administration, calendrics and engineering required facility with numbers. Numerical information was stored in the knots of <i><a href="/wiki/Quipu" title="Quipu">quipu</a></i> strings, allowing for compact storage of large numbers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NYT-20160102_96-0"><a href="#cite_note-NYT-20160102-96">[95]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcEwan2008183-185_97-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcEwan2008183-185-97">[96]</a></sup> These numbers were stored in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Base-10" title="Base-10">base-10</a> digits, the same base used by the Quechua language<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-98"><a href="#cite_note-98">[97]</a></sup> and in administrative and military units.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTED'Altroy2014233–34_88-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTED'Altroy2014233–34-88">[87]</a></sup> These numbers, stored in <i>quipu</i>, could be calculated on <i><a href="/wiki/Yupana" title="Yupana">yupanas</a></i>, grids with squares of positionally varying mathematical values, perhaps functioning as an <a href="/wiki/Abacus" title="Abacus">abacus</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-99"><a href="#cite_note-99">[98]</a></sup> Calculation was facilitated by moving piles of tokens, seeds or pebbles between compartments of the <i>yupana</i>. It is likely that Inca mathematics at least allowed division of integers into integers or fractions and multiplication of integers and fractions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcEwan2008185_100-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcEwan2008185-100">[99]</a></sup>
</p><p>According to mid-17th-century Jesuit chronicler Bernabé Cobo,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-101"><a href="#cite_note-101">[100]</a></sup> the Inca designated officials to perform accounting-related tasks. These officials were called quipo camayos. Study of khipu sample VA 42527 (Museum für Völkerkunde, Berlin)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-102"><a href="#cite_note-102">[101]</a></sup> revealed that the numbers arranged in calendrically significant patterns were used for agricultural purposes in the "farm account books" kept by the khipukamayuq (accountant or warehouse keeper) to facilitate the closing of accounting books.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-103"><a href="#cite_note-103">[102]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Tunics">Tunics</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Tupa-inca-tunic.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="944" data-file-width="800" decoding="async" height="260" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a2/Tupa-inca-tunic.png/220px-Tupa-inca-tunic.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a2/Tupa-inca-tunic.png/330px-Tupa-inca-tunic.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a2/Tupa-inca-tunic.png/440px-Tupa-inca-tunic.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Tupa-inca-tunic.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Inca tunic. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dumbarton_Oaks_Research_Library_and_Collection" title="Dumbarton Oaks Research Library and Collection">Dumbarton Oaks Research Library and Collection</a>.</div></div></div>
<p>Tunics were created by skilled Incan textile-makers as a piece of warm clothing, but they also symbolized cultural and political status and power. <i>Cumbi</i> was the fine, tapestry-woven woolen cloth that was produced and necessary for the creation of tunics. <i>Cumbi</i> was produced by specially-appointed women and men. Generally, textile-making was practiced by both men and women. As emphasized by certain historians, only with European conquest was it deemed that women would become the primary weavers in society, as opposed to Incan society where specialty textiles were produced by men and women equally.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:4_51-1"><a href="#cite_note-:4-51">[50]</a></sup>
</p><p>Complex patterns and designs were meant to convey information about order in Andean society as well as the Universe. Tunics could also symbolize one's relationship to ancient rulers or important ancestors. These textiles were frequently designed to represent the physical order of a society, for example, the flow of <a href="/wiki/Tribute" title="Tribute">tribute</a> within an empire. Many tunics have a "checkerboard effect" which is known as the <i>collcapata</i>. According to historians Kenneth Mills, William B. Taylor, and Sandra Lauderdale Graham, the <i>collcapata</i> patterns "seem to have expressed concepts of commonality, and, ultimately, unity of all ranks of people, representing a careful kind of foundation upon which the structure of Inkaic universalism was built." Rulers wore various tunics throughout the year, switching them out for different occasions and feasts.
</p><p>The symbols present within the tunics suggest the importance of "pictographic expression" within Inkan and other Andean societies far before the iconographies of the Spanish Christians.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-104"><a href="#cite_note-104">[103]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Uncu">Uncu</span></h4>
<p><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Uncu_(tunic)" title="Uncu (tunic)">Uncu</a> was a men's garment similar to a tunic. It was an upper-body garment of knee-length; Royals wore it with a mantle cloth called ''<a href="/wiki/Yacolla_(garment)" title="Yacolla (garment)">yacolla</a>.''<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-105"><a href="#cite_note-105">[104]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-106"><a href="#cite_note-106">[105]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span id="Ceramics.2C_precious_metals_and_textiles"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Ceramics,_precious_metals_and_textiles">Ceramics, precious metals and textiles</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Camelid_Conopa,_1470-1532,_36.683.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1214" data-file-width="1536" decoding="async" height="174" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5d/Camelid_Conopa%2C_1470-1532%2C_36.683.jpg/220px-Camelid_Conopa%2C_1470-1532%2C_36.683.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5d/Camelid_Conopa%2C_1470-1532%2C_36.683.jpg/330px-Camelid_Conopa%2C_1470-1532%2C_36.683.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5d/Camelid_Conopa%2C_1470-1532%2C_36.683.jpg/440px-Camelid_Conopa%2C_1470-1532%2C_36.683.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Camelid_Conopa,_1470-1532,_36.683.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Camelid Conopa, 1470–1532, <a href="/wiki/Brooklyn_Museum" title="Brooklyn Museum">Brooklyn Museum</a>, Small stone figurines, or <i>conopas</i>, of llamas and alpacas were the most common ritual effigies used in the highlands of Peru and Bolivia. These devotional objects were often buried in the animals' corrals to bring protection and prosperity to their owners and fertility to the herds. The cylindrical cavities in their backs were filled with offerings to the gods in the form of a mixture including animal fat, coca leaves, maize kernels and seashells.</div></div></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Ceramic_art" title="Ceramic art">Ceramics</a> were painted using the polychrome technique portraying numerous motifs including animals, birds, waves, felines (popular in the <a href="/wiki/Chav%C3%ADn_culture" title="Chavín culture">Chavin culture</a>) and geometric patterns found in the <a href="/wiki/Nazca" title="Nazca">Nazca</a> style of ceramics. In a culture without a written language, ceramics portrayed the basic scenes of everyday life, including the smelting of metals, relationships and scenes of tribal warfare. The most distinctive Inca ceramic objects are the Cusco bottles or "aryballos".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HerreraBerrin1997_107-0"><a href="#cite_note-HerreraBerrin1997-107">[106]</a></sup> Many of these pieces are on display in Lima in the <a href="/wiki/Larco_Museum" title="Larco Museum">Larco Archaeological Museum</a> and the National Museum of Archaeology, Anthropology and History.
</p><p>Almost all of the gold and silver work of the Incan empire was melted down by the conquistadors, and shipped back to Spain.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-108"><a href="#cite_note-108">[107]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Communication_and_medicine">Communication and medicine</span></h3>
<p>The Inca recorded information on assemblages of knotted strings, known as <a href="/wiki/Quipu" title="Quipu">Quipu</a>, although they can no longer be decoded. Originally it was thought that Quipu were used only as mnemonic devices or to record numerical data. Quipus are also believed to record history and literature.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcEwan2008183_109-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcEwan2008183-109">[108]</a></sup>
</p><p>The Inca made many discoveries in medicine.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-110"><a href="#cite_note-110">[109]</a></sup> They performed successful <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Trepanation" title="Trepanation">skull surgery</a>, by cutting holes in the skull to alleviate fluid buildup and inflammation caused by head wounds. Many skull surgeries performed by Inca surgeons were successful. Survival rates were 80–90%, compared to about 30% before Inca times.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-111"><a href="#cite_note-111">[110]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Coca">Coca</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Erythroxylum-coca-foliage2.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="908" data-file-width="1360" decoding="async" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/48/Erythroxylum-coca-foliage2.jpg/220px-Erythroxylum-coca-foliage2.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/48/Erythroxylum-coca-foliage2.jpg/330px-Erythroxylum-coca-foliage2.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/48/Erythroxylum-coca-foliage2.jpg/440px-Erythroxylum-coca-foliage2.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Erythroxylum-coca-foliage2.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Coca" title="Coca">Coca</a> leaves</div></div></div>
<p>The Incas revered the <a href="/wiki/Coca" title="Coca">coca</a> plant as sacred/magical. Its leaves were used in moderate amounts to lessen hunger and pain during work, but were mostly used for religious and health purposes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Boca_Raton_112-0"><a href="#cite_note-Boca_Raton-112">[111]</a></sup> The Spaniards took advantage of the effects of chewing coca leaves.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Boca_Raton_112-1"><a href="#cite_note-Boca_Raton-112">[111]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Chasqui" title="Chasqui">Chasqui</a>, messengers who ran throughout the empire to deliver messages, chewed coca leaves for extra energy. Coca leaves were also used as an <a href="/wiki/Anesthetic" title="Anesthetic">anaesthetic</a> during surgeries.
</p>
<h3><span id="Weapons.2C_armor_and_warfare"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Weapons,_armor_and_warfare">Weapons, armor and warfare</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Inca_army" title="Inca army">Inca army</a></div>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Sacsayhuaman_-_51188929520.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3456" data-file-width="5184" decoding="async" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/51/Sacsayhuaman_-_51188929520.jpg/220px-Sacsayhuaman_-_51188929520.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/51/Sacsayhuaman_-_51188929520.jpg/330px-Sacsayhuaman_-_51188929520.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/51/Sacsayhuaman_-_51188929520.jpg/440px-Sacsayhuaman_-_51188929520.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Sacsayhuaman_-_51188929520.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Sacsayhuam%C3%A1n" title="Sacsayhuamán">Sacsayhuamán</a>, the largest Inca <i><a href="/wiki/Pukara" title="Pukara">pukara</a></i> (largest Inca fortresses)</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Cabezas_de_Bronce.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="269" data-file-width="188" decoding="async" height="243" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bc/Cabezas_de_Bronce.jpg/170px-Cabezas_de_Bronce.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/b/bc/Cabezas_de_Bronce.jpg 1.5x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Cabezas_de_Bronce.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Copper" title="Copper">Copper</a> heads for maces.</div></div></div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Inca_army" title="Inca army">Inca army</a> was the most powerful at that time, because any ordinary villager or farmer could be recruited as a soldier as part of the <i><a href="/wiki/Mit%27a" title="Mit'a">mit'a</a></i> system of mandatory public service. Every able bodied male Inca of fighting age had to take part in war in some capacity at least once and to prepare for warfare again when needed. By the time the empire reached its largest size, every section of the empire contributed in setting up an army for war.
</p><p>The Incas had no iron or steel and their weapons were not much more effective than those of their opponents so they often defeated opponents by sheer force of numbers, or else by persuading them to surrender beforehand by offering generous terms.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AHE_113-0"><a href="#cite_note-AHE-113">[112]</a></sup> Inca weaponry included "hardwood spears launched using <a href="/wiki/Spear-thrower" title="Spear-thrower">throwers</a>, arrows, javelins, slings, the <a href="/wiki/Bolas" title="Bolas">bolas</a>, clubs, and maces with star-shaped heads made of copper or bronze".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AHE_113-1"><a href="#cite_note-AHE-113">[112]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MacQuarrie2008_114-0"><a href="#cite_note-MacQuarrie2008-114">[113]</a></sup> Rolling rocks downhill onto the enemy was a common strategy, taking advantage of the hilly terrain.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Parker2008_115-0"><a href="#cite_note-Parker2008-115">[114]</a></sup> Fighting was sometimes accompanied by drums and trumpets made of wood, shell or bone.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Stevenson1968_116-0"><a href="#cite_note-Stevenson1968-116">[115]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CoboHamilton1990_117-0"><a href="#cite_note-CoboHamilton1990-117">[116]</a></sup> Armor included:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AHE_113-2"><a href="#cite_note-AHE-113">[112]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Burland1968_118-0"><a href="#cite_note-Burland1968-118">[117]</a></sup>
</p>
<ul><li>Helmets made of wood, cane, or animal skin, often lined with copper or bronze; some were adorned with feathers</li>
<li>Round or square shields made from wood or hide</li>
<li>Cloth tunics padded with cotton and small wooden planks to protect the spine</li>
<li>Ceremonial metal breastplates, of copper, silver, and gold, have been found in burial sites, some of which may have also been used in battle.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SiversDesnoyers2012_119-0"><a href="#cite_note-SiversDesnoyers2012-119">[118]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-120"><a href="#cite_note-120">[119]</a></sup></li></ul>
<p>Roads allowed quick movement (on foot) for the Inca army and shelters called <i><a href="/wiki/Tambo_(Incan_structure)" title="Tambo (Incan structure)">tambo</a></i> and storage silos called <a href="/wiki/Qullqa" title="Qullqa">qullqas</a> were built one day's travelling distance from each other, so that an army on campaign could always be fed and rested. This can be seen in names of ruins such as <i>Ollantay Tambo</i>, or My Lord's Storehouse. These were set up so the Inca and his entourage would always have supplies (and possibly shelter) ready as they traveled.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Banner_of_the_Inca">Banner of the Inca</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Wiphala" title="Wiphala">Wiphala</a> and <a href="/wiki/Rainbow_flag#Andean_indigenism" title="Rainbow flag">Rainbow flag § Andean indigenism</a></div>
<p>Chronicles and references from the 16th and 17th centuries support the idea of a banner. However, it represented the Inca (emperor), not the empire.
</p><p>Francisco López de Jerez<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-121"><a href="#cite_note-121">[120]</a></sup> wrote in 1534:
</p>
<blockquote><p><span title="Spanish-language text"><span lang="es" style="font-style: normal;">... todos venían repartidos en sus escuadras con sus banderas y capitanes que los mandan, con tanto concierto como turcos.</span></span><br/>(<span title="English-language text"><span lang="en">... all of them came distributed into squads, with their flags and captains commanding them, as well-ordered as Turks.</span></span>) </p></blockquote>
<p>Chronicler Bernabé Cobo wrote:
</p>
<blockquote><p><span title="English-language text"><span lang="en">The royal standard or banner was a small square flag, ten or twelve spans around, made of cotton or wool cloth, placed on the end of a long staff, stretched and stiff such that it did not wave in the air and on it each king painted his arms and emblems, for each one chose different ones, though the sign of the Incas was the rainbow and two parallel snakes along the width with the tassel as a crown, which each king used to add for a badge or blazon those preferred, like a lion, an eagle and other figures.</span></span>
<br/>(<span title="Spanish-language text"><span lang="es" style="font-style: normal;">... el guión o estandarte real era una banderilla cuadrada y pequeña, de diez o doce palmos de ruedo, hecha de lienzo de algodón o de lana, iba puesta en el remate de una asta larga, tendida y tiesa, sin que ondease al aire, y en ella pintaba cada rey sus armas y divisas, porque cada uno las escogía diferentes, aunque las generales de los Incas eran el arco celeste y dos culebras tendidas a lo largo paralelas con la borda que le servía de corona, a las cuales solía añadir por divisa y blasón cada rey las que le parecía, como un león, un águila y otras figuras.</span></span>)<br/>-<small><b>Bernabé Cobo, <i>Historia del Nuevo Mundo</i></b> (1653)</small></p></blockquote>
<p><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Guaman_Poma" title="Guaman Poma">Guaman Poma</a>'s 1615 book, <i>El primer nueva corónica y buen gobierno</i>, shows numerous line drawings of Inca flags.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-122"><a href="#cite_note-122">[121]</a></sup> In his 1847 book <i>A History of the Conquest of Peru</i>, "<a href="/wiki/William_H._Prescott" title="William H. Prescott">William H. Prescott</a> ... says that in the Inca army each company had its particular banner and that the imperial standard, high above all, displayed the glittering device of the rainbow, the armorial ensign of the Incas."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-123"><a href="#cite_note-123">[122]</a></sup> A 1917 world flags book says the Inca "heir-apparent ... was entitled to display the royal standard of the rainbow in his military campaigns."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-124"><a href="#cite_note-124">[123]</a></sup>
</p><p>In modern times the <a href="/wiki/Rainbow_flag" title="Rainbow flag">rainbow flag</a> has been wrongly associated with the Tawantinsuyu and displayed as a symbol of Inca heritage by some groups in Peru and Bolivia. The city of Cusco also flies the Rainbow Flag, but as an official flag of the city. The Peruvian president <a href="/wiki/Alejandro_Toledo" title="Alejandro Toledo">Alejandro Toledo</a> (2001–2006) flew the Rainbow Flag in <a href="/wiki/Lima" title="Lima">Lima</a>'s presidential palace. However, according to Peruvian historiography, the Inca Empire never had a flag. Peruvian historian <a href="/wiki/Mar%C3%ADa_Rostworowski" title="María Rostworowski">María Rostworowski</a> said, "I bet my life, the Inca never had that flag, it never existed, no chronicler mentioned it".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-125"><a href="#cite_note-125">[124]</a></sup> Also, to the Peruvian newspaper <i>El Comercio</i>, the flag dates to the first decades of the 20th century,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-La_Bandera_del_Tahuantisuyo_126-0"><a href="#cite_note-La_Bandera_del_Tahuantisuyo-126">[125]</a></sup> and even the <a href="/wiki/Congress_of_the_Republic_of_Peru" title="Congress of the Republic of Peru">Congress of the Republic of Peru</a> has determined that flag is a fake by citing the conclusion of National Academy of Peruvian History:
</p>
<blockquote><p>"The official use of the wrongly called 'Tawantinsuyu flag' is a mistake. In the Pre-Hispanic Andean World there did not exist the concept of a flag, it did not belong to their historic context".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-La_Bandera_del_Tahuantisuyo_126-1"><a href="#cite_note-La_Bandera_del_Tahuantisuyo-126">[125]</a></sup>
<br/><b>National Academy of Peruvian History</b></p></blockquote>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Adaptations_to_altitude">Adaptations to altitude</span></h2>
<p>The people of the Andes, including the Incas, were able to adapt to high-altitude living through successful acclimatization, which is characterized by increasing oxygen supply to the blood tissues. For the native living in the Andean highlands, this was achieved through the development of a larger lung capacity, and an increase in red blood cell counts, hemoglobin concentration, and capillary beds.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-127"><a href="#cite_note-127">[126]</a></sup>
</p><p>Compared to other humans, the Andeans had slower heart rates, almost one-third larger <a href="/wiki/Lung" title="Lung">lung</a> capacity, about 2 L (4 pints) more blood volume and double the amount of <a href="/wiki/Hemoglobin" title="Hemoglobin">hemoglobin</a>, which transfers <a href="/wiki/Oxygen" title="Oxygen">oxygen</a> from the lungs to the rest of the body. While the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Conquistadors" title="Conquistadors">Conquistadors</a> may have been taller, the Inca had the advantage of coping with the extraordinary altitude.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-128"><a href="#cite_note-128">[127]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Tibetan_people" title="Tibetan people">Tibetans</a> in Asia living in the <a href="/wiki/Himalayas" title="Himalayas">Himalayas</a> are also adapted to living in high-altitudes, although the adaptation is different from that of the Andeans.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gibbons_129-0"><a href="#cite_note-Gibbons-129">[128]</a></sup>
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="References">References</span></h2>
